     <p>The <b>University of North Carolina at Chapel Hill</b> (also known as <b>UNC</b>, <b>UNC-Chapel Hill</b>, <b>Chapel Hill</b>, <b>North Carolina</b>, or simply <b>Carolina</b>)<sup id="cite_ref-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-14"><span>[</span>14<span>]</span></a></sup> is a <a href="/wiki/Coeducation" title="Coeducation" class="mw-redirect">coeducational</a> <a href="/wiki/Public_university" title="Public university">public</a> research university located in <a href="/wiki/Chapel_Hill,_North_Carolina" title="Chapel Hill, North Carolina">Chapel Hill, North Carolina</a>, United States. It is the <a href="/wiki/Flagship_campus" title="Flagship campus" class="mw-redirect">flagship</a> institution of the <a href="/wiki/University_of_North_Carolina" title="University of North Carolina">University of North Carolina</a> and the second largest university in North Carolina. North Carolina has been consistently ranked among the highest ranked universities in the United States and is one of the original eight <a href="/wiki/Public_Ivy" title="Public Ivy">Public Ivy</a> schools that provide an <a href="/wiki/Ivy_League" title="Ivy League">Ivy League</a> experience for a public schooling price. After being chartered in 1789, the university first began enrolling students in 1795, which allows it to be one of three schools to claim the title of the <a href="/wiki/Oldest_public_university_in_the_United_States" title="Oldest public university in the United States">oldest public university in the United States</a>.</p> 
     <p>The first public institution of higher education in North Carolina, the school opened its doors to students on February 12, 1795. The university offers degrees in over 70 courses of study through fourteen colleges and the College of Arts and Sciences. All undergraduates receive a <a href="/wiki/Liberal_arts" title="Liberal arts" class="mw-redirect">liberal arts</a> education and have the option to pursue a major within the professional schools of the university or within the College of Arts and Sciences from the time they obtain junior status. Under the leadership of President <a href="/wiki/Kemp_Plummer_Battle" title="Kemp Plummer Battle" class="mw-redirect">Kemp Plummer Battle</a>, in 1877 North Carolina became coeducational, while desegregation ended when African-American graduate students were admitted under Chancellor Robert Burton House in 1951.<sup id="cite_ref-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-15"><span>[</span>15<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-16"><span>[</span>16<span>]</span></a></sup> In 1952, North Carolina opened its own hospital, <a href="/wiki/UNC_Health_Care" title="UNC Health Care">UNC Health Care</a>, for research and treatment, and has since specialized in <a href="/wiki/Cancer" title="Cancer">cancer care</a>. The school's students, <a href="/wiki/Alumnus" title="Alumnus">alumni</a>, and sports teams are known as &quot;<a href="/wiki/North_Carolina_Tar_Heels" title="North Carolina Tar Heels">Tar Heels</a>&quot;.</p> 
     <p>The campus of North Carolina is located in Chapel Hill, North Carolina, a university town. The campus covers a rather small 729 acres (3&nbsp;km<sup>2</sup>) over Chapel Hill's downtown area, encompassing places like the <a href="/wiki/Morehead_Planetarium" title="Morehead Planetarium" class="mw-redirect">Morehead Planetarium</a> and the many stores and shops located on <a href="/wiki/Franklin_Street_(Chapel_Hill)" title="Franklin Street (Chapel Hill)">Franklin Street</a>. Students can participate in over 550 officially recognized student organizations. The student-run newspaper <i><a href="/wiki/The_Daily_Tar_Heel" title="The Daily Tar Heel">The Daily Tar Heel</a></i> has won national awards for collegiate media, while the <a href="/wiki/Campus_radio" title="Campus radio">student radio</a> station <a href="/wiki/WXYC" title="WXYC">WXYC</a> provided the world's first internet radio broadcast. North Carolina is one of the charter members of the <a href="/wiki/Atlantic_Coast_Conference" title="Atlantic Coast Conference">Atlantic Coast Conference</a>, which was founded on June 14, 1953. Competing athletically as the <a href="/wiki/North_Carolina_Tar_Heels" title="North Carolina Tar Heels">Tar Heels</a>, North Carolina has achieved great success in sports, most notably in <a href="/wiki/North_Carolina_Tar_Heels_men%27s_basketball" title="North Carolina Tar Heels men's basketball">men's basketball</a> and <a href="/wiki/North_Carolina_Tar_Heels_women%27s_soccer" title="North Carolina Tar Heels women's soccer">women's soccer</a>.</p> 
     <table id="toc" class="toc"> 
      <tbody>

     <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection">[<a href="/w/index.php?title=University_of_North_Carolina_at_Chapel_Hill&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: History">edit</a>]</span></h2> 
     <div class="rellink relarticle mainarticle">
      Main article: 
      <a href="/wiki/History_of_the_University_of_North_Carolina_at_Chapel_Hill" title="History of the University of North Carolina at Chapel Hill">History of the University of North Carolina at Chapel Hill</a>
     </div> 
     <div class="thumb tright"> 
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a href="/wiki/File:UNC-6-1819.pdf" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3c/UNC-6-1819.pdf/page1-220px-UNC-6-1819.pdf.jpg" width="220" height="403" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3c/UNC-6-1819.pdf/page1-330px-UNC-6-1819.pdf.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3c/UNC-6-1819.pdf/page1-440px-UNC-6-1819.pdf.jpg 2x" /></a> 
       <div class="thumbcaption"> 
        <div class="magnify">
         <a href="/wiki/File:UNC-6-1819.pdf" class="internal" title="Enlarge"><img src="//bits.wikimedia.org/static-1.22wmf8/skins/common/images/magnify-clip.png" width="15" height="11" alt="" /></a>
        </div> UNC course catalog from June 1819
       </div> 
      </div> 
     </div> 
     <p><a href="/wiki/Charter" title="Charter">Chartered</a> by the <a href="/wiki/North_Carolina_General_Assembly" title="North Carolina General Assembly">North Carolina General Assembly</a> on December 11, 1789, the university's cornerstone was laid on October 12, 1793, near the ruins of a chapel, chosen because of its central location within the state.<sup id="cite_ref-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-17"><span>[</span>17<span>]</span></a></sup> Beginning instruction of undergraduates in 1795, UNC is one of the <a href="/wiki/Oldest_public_university_in_the_United_States" title="Oldest public university in the United States">oldest public universities in the United States</a> and the only such institution to confer degrees in the eighteenth century.<sup id="cite_ref-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-18"><span>[</span>18<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19"><span>[</span>19<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <p>During the <a href="/wiki/American_Civil_War" title="American Civil War">Civil War</a>, <a href="/wiki/Governor_of_North_Carolina" title="Governor of North Carolina">North Carolina Governor</a> <a href="/wiki/David_Lowry_Swain" title="David Lowry Swain">David Lowry Swain</a> persuaded <a href="/wiki/President_of_the_Confederate_States_of_America" title="President of the Confederate States of America">Confederate President</a> <a href="/wiki/Jefferson_Davis" title="Jefferson Davis">Jefferson Davis</a> to exempt some students from the draft, so the university was one of the few in the <a href="/wiki/Confederate_States_of_America" title="Confederate States of America">Confederacy</a> that managed to stay open.<sup id="cite_ref-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20"><span>[</span>20<span>]</span></a></sup> However, <a href="/wiki/Chapel_Hill,_North_Carolina" title="Chapel Hill, North Carolina">Chapel Hill</a> suffered the loss of more of its population during the war than any village in the South, and when student numbers did not recover, the university was forced to close during <a href="/wiki/Reconstruction_era_of_the_United_States" title="Reconstruction era of the United States" class="mw-redirect">Reconstruction</a> from December 1, 1870 until September 6, 1875.<sup id="cite_ref-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-21"><span>[</span>21<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <p>Despite initial skepticism from university President <a href="/wiki/Frank_Porter_Graham" title="Frank Porter Graham">Frank Porter Graham</a>, on March 27, 1931, legislation was passed to group UNC with the <a href="/wiki/North_Carolina_State_University" title="North Carolina State University">State College of Agriculture and Engineering</a> and the <a href="/wiki/University_of_North_Carolina_at_Greensboro" title="University of North Carolina at Greensboro">Women's College</a> to form the Consolidated University of North Carolina.<sup id="cite_ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22"><span>[</span>22<span>]</span></a></sup> In 1963, the consolidated university was made fully <a href="/wiki/Coeducational" title="Coeducational" class="mw-redirect">coeducational</a>, although most women still attended the Women's College for their first two years, transferring to Chapel Hill as a junior, since freshmen were required to live on campus and there was only one women's dorm. As a result, the Women's College was renamed the &quot;<a href="/wiki/University_of_North_Carolina_at_Greensboro" title="University of North Carolina at Greensboro">University of North Carolina at Greensboro</a>&quot;, and the University of North Carolina became the &quot;University of North Carolina at Chapel Hill.&quot;<sup id="cite_ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23"><span>[</span>23<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24"><span>[</span>24<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25"><span>[</span>25<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26"><span>[</span>26<span>]</span></a></sup> In 1955, UNC officially desegregated its undergraduate divisions.<sup id="cite_ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27"><span>[</span>27<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <div class="thumb tright"> 
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a href="/wiki/File:Silent_Sam.jpg" class="image"><img alt="A statue of a soldier with three people walking around the base." src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a7/Silent_Sam.jpg/220px-Silent_Sam.jpg" width="220" height="232" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a7/Silent_Sam.jpg/330px-Silent_Sam.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a7/Silent_Sam.jpg/440px-Silent_Sam.jpg 2x" /></a> 
       <div class="thumbcaption"> 
        <div class="magnify">
         <a href="/wiki/File:Silent_Sam.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"><img src="//bits.wikimedia.org/static-1.22wmf8/skins/common/images/magnify-clip.png" width="15" height="11" alt="" /></a>
        </div> 
        <a href="/wiki/Confederate_States_of_America" title="Confederate States of America">Confederate</a> soldier 
        <a href="/wiki/Silent_Sam" title="Silent Sam">Silent Sam</a>, University of North Carolina at Chapel Hill by sculptor 
        <a href="/wiki/John_Wilson_(sculptor)" title="John Wilson (sculptor)" class="mw-redirect">John Wilson</a>.
       </div> 
      </div> 
     </div> 
     <p>During World War II, UNC at Chapel Hill was one of 131 colleges and universities nationally that took part in the <a href="/wiki/V-12_Navy_College_Training_Program" title="V-12 Navy College Training Program">V-12 Navy College Training Program</a> which offered students a path to a Navy commission.<sup id="cite_ref-unorthcarolina-v-12_28-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-unorthcarolina-v-12-28"><span>[</span>28<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <p>During the 1960s, the campus was the location of significant political protest. Prior to the passage of the <a href="/wiki/Civil_Rights_Act_of_1964" title="Civil Rights Act of 1964">Civil Rights Act of 1964</a>, protests about local <a href="/wiki/Racial_segregation" title="Racial segregation">racial segregation</a> which began quietly in <a href="/wiki/Franklin_Street_(Chapel_Hill)" title="Franklin Street (Chapel Hill)">Franklin Street</a> restaurants led to mass demonstrations and disturbance.<sup id="cite_ref-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-29"><span>[</span>29<span>]</span></a></sup> The climate of civil unrest prompted the 1963 <a href="/wiki/North_Carolina_Speaker_Ban" title="North Carolina Speaker Ban">Speaker Ban Law</a> prohibiting speeches by communists on state campuses in North Carolina.<sup id="cite_ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30"><span>[</span>30<span>]</span></a></sup> The law was immediately criticized by university Chancellor <a href="/wiki/William_Brantley_Aycock" title="William Brantley Aycock">William Brantley Aycock</a> and university President <a href="/wiki/William_Friday" title="William Friday" class="mw-redirect">William Friday</a>, but was not reviewed by the North Carolina General Assembly until 1965.<sup id="cite_ref-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-31"><span>[</span>31<span>]</span></a></sup> Small amendments to allow &quot;infrequent&quot; visits failed to placate the student body, especially when the university's board of trustees overruled new Chancellor Paul Frederick Sharp's decision to allow speaking invitations to <a href="/wiki/Marxist" title="Marxist" class="mw-redirect">Marxist</a> speaker <a href="/wiki/Herbert_Aptheker" title="Herbert Aptheker">Herbert Aptheker</a> and civil liberties activist <a href="/wiki/Frank_Wilkinson" title="Frank Wilkinson">Frank Wilkinson</a>; however, the two speakers came to Chapel Hill anyway. Wilkinson spoke off campus, while more than 1,500 students viewed Aptheker's speech across a low campus wall at the edge of campus, christened &quot;Dan Moore's Wall&quot; by <i><a href="/wiki/The_Daily_Tar_Heel" title="The Daily Tar Heel">The Daily Tar Heel</a></i> for Governor <a href="/wiki/Dan_K._Moore" title="Dan K. Moore">Dan K. Moore</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32"><span>[</span>32<span>]</span></a></sup> A group of UNC students, led by Student Body President Paul Dickson, filed a lawsuit in <a href="/wiki/United_States_federal_courts" title="United States federal courts">U.S. federal court</a>, and on February 20, 1968, the Speaker Ban Law was struck down.<sup id="cite_ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33"><span>[</span>33<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <p>From the late 1990s onward, UNC expanded rapidly with a 15% increase in total student population to more than 28,000 by 2007. This was accompanied by the construction of new facilities, funded in part by the &quot;Carolina First&quot; fundraising campaign and an <a href="/wiki/Financial_endowment" title="Financial endowment">endowment</a> that increased fourfold to over $2&nbsp;billion in just ten years.<sup id="cite_ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34"><span>[</span>34<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35"><span>[</span>35<span>]</span></a></sup> Professor <a href="/wiki/Oliver_Smithies" title="Oliver Smithies">Oliver Smithies</a> was awarded the <a href="/wiki/Nobel_Prize_in_Medicine" title="Nobel Prize in Medicine" class="mw-redirect">Nobel Prize in Medicine</a> in 2007 for his work in <a href="/wiki/Genetics" title="Genetics">genetics</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-36"><span>[</span>36<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <p>Notable <a href="/wiki/Leaders_of_the_University_of_North_Carolina_at_Chapel_Hill" title="Leaders of the University of North Carolina at Chapel Hill" class="mw-redirect">leaders of the university</a> include the 26th Governor of North Carolina, <a href="/wiki/David_Lowry_Swain" title="David Lowry Swain">David Lowry Swain</a> (president 1835?1868); and <a href="/wiki/Edwin_Alderman" title="Edwin Alderman">Edwin Anderson Alderman</a> (1896?1900), who was also president of <a href="/wiki/Tulane_University" title="Tulane University">Tulane University</a> and the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Virginia" title="University of Virginia">University of Virginia</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37"><span>[</span>37<span>]</span></a></sup> The current chancellor is <a href="/wiki/Carol_Folt" title="Carol Folt">Carol Folt</a>, the first woman to hold the post.<sup id="cite_ref-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-38"><span>[</span>38<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Campus">Campus</span><span class="mw-editsection">[<a href="/w/index.php?title=University_of_North_Carolina_at_Chapel_Hill&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Campus">edit</a>]</span></h2> 
     <div class="thumb tleft"> 
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:262px;">
       <a href="/wiki/File:Morehead-Planetarium-at-UNC.jpg" class="image"><img alt=" A brick building with a rusted dome and ionic columns." src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/16/Morehead-Planetarium-at-UNC.jpg/260px-Morehead-Planetarium-at-UNC.jpg" width="260" height="173" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/16/Morehead-Planetarium-at-UNC.jpg/390px-Morehead-Planetarium-at-UNC.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/16/Morehead-Planetarium-at-UNC.jpg/520px-Morehead-Planetarium-at-UNC.jpg 2x" /></a> 
       <div class="thumbcaption"> 
        <div class="magnify">
         <a href="/wiki/File:Morehead-Planetarium-at-UNC.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"><img src="//bits.wikimedia.org/static-1.22wmf8/skins/common/images/magnify-clip.png" width="15" height="11" alt="" /></a>
        </div> The 
        <a href="/wiki/Morehead_Planetarium" title="Morehead Planetarium" class="mw-redirect">Morehead Planetarium</a>, designed by 
        <a href="/wiki/Eggers_%26_Higgins" title="Eggers &amp; Higgins">Eggers &amp; Higgins</a>, first opened in 1949.
        <sup id="cite_ref-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-39"><span>[</span>39<span>]</span></a></sup>
       </div> 
      </div> 
     </div> 
     <p>UNC's 729-acre (3.0&nbsp;km<sup>2</sup>) campus is dominated by two central <a href="/wiki/Quadrangle_(architecture)" title="Quadrangle (architecture)">quads</a>: Polk Place and McCorkle Place.<sup id="cite_ref-beautiful_40-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-beautiful-40"><span>[</span>40<span>]</span></a></sup> Polk Place is named after North Carolina native and university alumnus President <a href="/wiki/James_K._Polk" title="James K. Polk">James K. Polk</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-41"><span>[</span>41<span>]</span></a></sup> and McCorkle Place is named in honor of Samuel Eusebius McCorkle, the original author of the <a href="/wiki/Bill_(proposed_law)" title="Bill (proposed law)" class="mw-redirect">bill</a> requesting the university's <a href="/wiki/Charter" title="Charter">charter</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-42"><span>[</span>42<span>]</span></a></sup> Adjacent to Polk Place is a sunken brick courtyard known as the Pit where students will gather, often engaging in lively debate with speakers such as the <a href="/wiki/Gary_Birdsong" title="Gary Birdsong">Pit Preacher</a>. The Morehead?Patterson Bell Tower, located in the heart of campus, tolls the quarter-hour. In 1999, UNC was one of sixteen recipients of the <a href="/wiki/American_Society_of_Landscape_Architects" title="American Society of Landscape Architects">American Society of Landscape Architects</a> Medallion Awards and was identified as one of 50 college or university &quot;works of art&quot; by T.A. Gaines in his book <i>The Campus as a Work of Art</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-beautiful_40-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-beautiful-40"><span>[</span>40<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-43"><span>[</span>43<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <p>The university's campus is informally divided into three regions, usually referred to as &quot;north campus,&quot; &quot;middle campus,&quot; and &quot;south campus.&quot; North campus includes the two quads along with the Pit, Frank Porter Graham Student Union, and the Davis, House, and Wilson libraries. Almost all classrooms are located in north campus along with several undergraduate residence halls.<sup id="cite_ref-campusmap_44-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-campusmap-44"><span>[</span>44<span>]</span></a></sup> Middle campus includes <a href="/wiki/Fetzer_Field" title="Fetzer Field">Fetzer Field</a> and <a href="/wiki/Woollen_Gymnasium" title="Woollen Gymnasium">Woollen Gymnasium</a> along with the Student Recreation Center, <a href="/wiki/Kenan_Memorial_Stadium" title="Kenan Memorial Stadium">Kenan Memorial Stadium</a>, Irwin Belk outdoor track, <a href="/wiki/Eddie_Smith_Field_House" title="Eddie Smith Field House">Eddie Smith Field House</a>, <a href="/wiki/Boshamer_Stadium" title="Boshamer Stadium">Boshamer Stadium</a>, <a href="/wiki/Carmichael_Auditorium" title="Carmichael Auditorium" class="mw-redirect">Carmichael Auditorium</a>, <a href="/wiki/The_Sonja_Haynes_Stone_Center_for_Black_Culture_and_History" title="The Sonja Haynes Stone Center for Black Culture and History">the Sonja Haynes Stone Center for Black Culture and History</a>, School of Government, <a href="/wiki/University_of_North_Carolina_School_of_Law" title="University of North Carolina School of Law">School of Law</a>, George Watts Hill Alumni Center, Ram's Head complex (with a dining hall, parking garage, grocery store, and gymnasium), and various residence halls.<sup id="cite_ref-campusmap_44-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-campusmap-44"><span>[</span>44<span>]</span></a></sup> South campus includes the <a href="/wiki/Dean_Smith_Center" title="Dean Smith Center">Dean Smith Center</a> for men's basketball, Koury Natatorium, <a href="/wiki/University_of_North_Carolina_School_of_Medicine" title="University of North Carolina School of Medicine">School of Medicine</a>, <a href="/wiki/UNC_Health_Care" title="UNC Health Care">UNC Hospitals</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kenan%E2%80%93Flagler_Business_School" title="Kenan?Flagler Business School">Kenan?Flagler Business School</a>, and the newest student residence halls.<sup id="cite_ref-campusmap_44-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-campusmap-44"><span>[</span>44<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <div class="thumb tright"> 
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a href="/wiki/File:UNC_location_seal.JPG" class="image"><img alt=" A seal for a school that contains the name and the date of founding." src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c7/UNC_location_seal.JPG/220px-UNC_location_seal.JPG" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c7/UNC_location_seal.JPG/330px-UNC_location_seal.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c7/UNC_location_seal.JPG/440px-UNC_location_seal.JPG 2x" /></a> 
       <div class="thumbcaption"> 
        <div class="magnify">
         <a href="/wiki/File:UNC_location_seal.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"><img src="//bits.wikimedia.org/static-1.22wmf8/skins/common/images/magnify-clip.png" width="15" height="11" alt="" /></a>
        </div> A representation of the university seal, located in front of South Building. Dedicated by the class of 1989, it marks UNC's exact geographic location.
       </div> 
      </div> 
     </div> 
     <p>A new <a href="/wiki/Satellite_campus" title="Satellite campus">satellite campus</a>, Carolina North, to be built on the site of <a href="/wiki/Horace_Williams_Airport" title="Horace Williams Airport">Horace Williams Airport</a> was approved in 2007. This is planned to be primarily a <a href="/wiki/Science_park" title="Science park">research park</a> with expanded science facilities, but will also add classrooms and residence halls to cope with future increases in student population.<sup id="cite_ref-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-45"><span>[</span>45<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-46"><span>[</span>46<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Sustainability">Sustainability</span><span class="mw-editsection">[<a href="/w/index.php?title=University_of_North_Carolina_at_Chapel_Hill&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Sustainability">edit</a>]</span></h3> 
     <p>The principles of sustainability have been integrated throughout much of UNC-Chapel Hill. In the area of green building, the university requires that all new projects meet the requirements for <a href="/wiki/Leadership_in_Energy_and_Environmental_Design" title="Leadership in Energy and Environmental Design">LEED</a> Silver certification and is in the process of building the first building in North Carolina to receive LEED Platinum status.<sup id="cite_ref-UNC_Sustainability:_Buildings_47-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-UNC_Sustainability:_Buildings-47"><span>[</span>47<span>]</span></a></sup> UNC's award-winning co-generation facility produces one-fourth of the electricity and all of the steam used on campus.<sup id="cite_ref-UNC_Sustainability:_Energy_at_UNC_48-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-UNC_Sustainability:_Energy_at_UNC-48"><span>[</span>48<span>]</span></a></sup> In 2006, the university and the <a href="/wiki/Chapel_Hill,_North_Carolina" title="Chapel Hill, North Carolina">Town of Chapel Hill</a> jointly agreed to reduce greenhouse gas emissions 60% by 2050, becoming the first town-gown partnership in the country to do so.<sup id="cite_ref-UNC_Sustainability:_Institutionalizing_Sustainability_49-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-UNC_Sustainability:_Institutionalizing_Sustainability-49"><span>[</span>49<span>]</span></a></sup> Through these efforts, the university achieved a ?A?? grade on the Sustainable Endowment Institute's College Sustainability Report Card 2010.<sup id="cite_ref-Sustainable_Endowments_Institute_Report_Card_50-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sustainable_Endowments_Institute_Report_Card-50"><span>[</span>50<span>]</span></a></sup> Only 14 out of 300 universities received a higher score than this.</p> 
     <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Old_Well_and_McCorkle_Place">Old Well and McCorkle Place</span><span class="mw-editsection">[<a href="/w/index.php?title=University_of_North_Carolina_at_Chapel_Hill&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Old Well and McCorkle Place">edit</a>]</span></h3> 
     <div class="thumb tright"> 
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a href="/wiki/File:The_%22Old_Well%22,_center_of_campus,_University_of_North_Carolina,_Chapel_Hill.jpg" class="image"><img alt="A white colored well is seen through the trees and pink bushes." src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/45/The_%22Old_Well%22%2C_center_of_campus%2C_University_of_North_Carolina%2C_Chapel_Hill.jpg/220px-The_%22Old_Well%22%2C_center_of_campus%2C_University_of_North_Carolina%2C_Chapel_Hill.jpg" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/45/The_%22Old_Well%22%2C_center_of_campus%2C_University_of_North_Carolina%2C_Chapel_Hill.jpg/330px-The_%22Old_Well%22%2C_center_of_campus%2C_University_of_North_Carolina%2C_Chapel_Hill.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/45/The_%22Old_Well%22%2C_center_of_campus%2C_University_of_North_Carolina%2C_Chapel_Hill.jpg/440px-The_%22Old_Well%22%2C_center_of_campus%2C_University_of_North_Carolina%2C_Chapel_Hill.jpg 2x" /></a> 
       <div class="thumbcaption"> 
        <div class="magnify">
         <a href="/wiki/File:The_%22Old_Well%22,_center_of_campus,_University_of_North_Carolina,_Chapel_Hill.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"><img src="//bits.wikimedia.org/static-1.22wmf8/skins/common/images/magnify-clip.png" width="15" height="11" alt="" /></a>
        </div> The 
        <a href="/wiki/Old_Well" title="Old Well">Old Well</a>, a symbol of the University, stands at the heart of campus.
       </div> 
      </div> 
     </div> 
     <p>The most enduring symbol of the university is the <a href="/wiki/Old_Well" title="Old Well">Old Well</a>, a small neoclassical <a href="/wiki/Rotunda_(architecture)" title="Rotunda (architecture)">rotunda</a> based on the Temple of Love in the <a href="/wiki/Gardens_of_Versailles" title="Gardens of Versailles">Gardens of Versailles</a>, in the same location as the original well that provided water for the school.<sup id="cite_ref-landmarks_51-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-landmarks-51"><span>[</span>51<span>]</span></a></sup> The well stands at the south end of McCorkle Place, the northern quad, between two of the campus's oldest buildings, <a href="/wiki/Old_East" title="Old East">Old East</a>, and Old West. Also located in McCorkle Place is the <a href="/wiki/Davie_Poplar" title="Davie Poplar">Davie Poplar</a> tree under which the university's founder, <a href="/wiki/William_Richardson_Davie" title="William Richardson Davie">William Richardson Davie</a>, supposedly selected the location for the university. The legend of the Davie Poplar says that if the tree falls, so will UNC.<sup id="cite_ref-poplar_52-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-poplar-52"><span>[</span>52<span>]</span></a></sup> Because of the tree's questionable health from damage caused by severe weather such as <a href="/wiki/Hurricane_Fran" title="Hurricane Fran">Hurricane Fran</a> in 1996, the university has planted two genetic clones nearby called Davie Poplar Jr. and Davie Poplar III.<sup id="cite_ref-poplar_52-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-poplar-52"><span>[</span>52<span>]</span></a></sup> The second clone, Davie Poplar III, was planted in conjunction with the university's <a href="//en.wiktionary.org/wiki/bicentennial" class="extiw" title="wikt:bicentennial">bicentennial</a> celebration in 1993 by <a href="/wiki/President_of_the_United_States" title="President of the United States">President</a> <a href="/wiki/Bill_Clinton" title="Bill Clinton">Bill Clinton</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-53"><span>[</span>53<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-reflections_54-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-reflections-54"><span>[</span>54<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <div class="thumb tleft"> 
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;">
       <a href="/wiki/File:Morehead-Patterson_Bell_Tower_at_dusk.jpg" class="image"><img alt="A bell tower in the night with lit up archways at the base and a clock near the top of the tower." src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2d/Morehead-Patterson_Bell_Tower_at_dusk.jpg/170px-Morehead-Patterson_Bell_Tower_at_dusk.jpg" width="170" height="320" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2d/Morehead-Patterson_Bell_Tower_at_dusk.jpg/255px-Morehead-Patterson_Bell_Tower_at_dusk.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2d/Morehead-Patterson_Bell_Tower_at_dusk.jpg/340px-Morehead-Patterson_Bell_Tower_at_dusk.jpg 2x" /></a> 
       <div class="thumbcaption"> 
        <div class="magnify">
         <a href="/wiki/File:Morehead-Patterson_Bell_Tower_at_dusk.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"><img src="//bits.wikimedia.org/static-1.22wmf8/skins/common/images/magnify-clip.png" width="15" height="11" alt="" /></a>
        </div> The Morehead?Patterson Bell Tower was completed in 1931 and stands 172 feet tall.
        <sup id="cite_ref-unc.edu_55-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-unc.edu-55"><span>[</span>55<span>]</span></a></sup>
       </div> 
      </div> 
     </div> 
     <p>Another university landmark is the Confederate monument, known to students as <a href="/wiki/Silent_Sam" title="Silent Sam">Silent Sam</a>, erected to commemorate UNC students who died fighting for the <a href="/wiki/Confederate_States_Army" title="Confederate States Army">Confederacy</a>. The statue has at times been dogged by controversy, some critics claiming that the monument invokes memories of racism and slavery, while others counter that &quot;Silent Sam&quot; is simply historical and a part of the rich heritage of the <a href="/wiki/Southern_United_States" title="Southern United States">South</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-56"><span>[</span>56<span>]</span></a></sup> The statue depicts a soldier armed with a rifle, but lacking a cartridge box. Thus, Silent Sam does not carry any ammunition and is a &quot;benign&quot; soldier. The statue was erected in 1913 by the United Daughters of the Confederacy to honor the school's Confederate heroes.<sup id="cite_ref-The_Weiss_Urban_Livability_Program_57-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-The_Weiss_Urban_Livability_Program-57"><span>[</span>57<span>]</span></a></sup> The student members of the university's <a href="/wiki/Dialectic_and_Philanthropic_Societies" title="Dialectic and Philanthropic Societies">Dialectic and Philanthropic Societies</a> are not allowed to walk on the grass of McCorkle Place out of respect for the unknown resting place of <a href="/wiki/Joseph_Caldwell" title="Joseph Caldwell">Joseph Caldwell</a>, the university's first president.<sup id="cite_ref-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-58"><span>[</span>58<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <p>The Morehead?Patterson bell tower was commissioned by <a href="/wiki/John_Motley_Morehead_III" title="John Motley Morehead III">John Motley Morehead III</a>, the benefactor of the prestigious <a href="/wiki/Morehead_Scholarship" title="Morehead Scholarship" class="mw-redirect">Morehead Scholarship</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-59" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-59"><span>[</span>59<span>]</span></a></sup> The hedge and surrounding landscape was designed by <a href="/wiki/William_Chambers_Coker" title="William Chambers Coker">William C. Coker</a>, botany professor and creator of the <a href="/wiki/Coker_Arboretum" title="Coker Arboretum">campus arboretum</a>. Traditionally, seniors have the opportunity to climb the tower a few days prior to May commencement.<sup id="cite_ref-unc.edu_55-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-unc.edu-55"><span>[</span>55<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <p>The historic <a href="/wiki/Playmakers_Theatre" title="Playmakers Theatre">Playmakers Theatre</a> is located on Cameron Avenue between McCorkle Place and Polk Place. It was designed by <a href="/wiki/Alexander_Jackson_Davis" title="Alexander Jackson Davis">Alexander Jackson Davis</a>, the same architect who renovated the northern fa&ccedil;ade of <a href="/wiki/Old_East" title="Old East">Old East</a> in 1844.<sup id="cite_ref-60" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-60"><span>[</span>60<span>]</span></a></sup> The east-facing building was completed in 1851 and initially served as a library and as a ballroom. It was originally named Smith Hall after North Carolina Governor <a href="/wiki/Benjamin_Smith_(North_Carolina)" title="Benjamin Smith (North Carolina)">General Benjamin Smith</a>, who was a special aide to <a href="/wiki/George_Washington" title="George Washington">George Washington</a> during the <a href="/wiki/American_Revolutionary_War" title="American Revolutionary War">American Revolutionary War</a> and was an early benefactor to the university.<sup id="cite_ref-61" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-61"><span>[</span>61<span>]</span></a></sup> When the library moved to Hill Hall in 1907, the School of Law occupied Smith Hall until 1923. In 1925, the structure was renovated and used as a stage by the university theater group, the Carolina Playmakers. It has remained a theater to the present day. <a href="/wiki/Louis_Round_Wilson" title="Louis Round Wilson">Louis Round Wilson</a> wrote in 1957 that Playmakers Theatre is the &quot;architectural gem of the campus.&quot;<sup id="cite_ref-62" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-62"><span>[</span>62<span>]</span></a></sup> Playmakers Theatre was declared a <a href="/wiki/National_Historic_Landmark" title="National Historic Landmark">National Historic Landmark</a> in 1973.<sup id="cite_ref-63" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-63"><span>[</span>63<span>]</span></a></sup> Today, the building is a venue for student drama productions, concerts, and events sponsored by academic departments. In 2006, the university began a renovation of Playmakers Theatre, which has included an exterior paint job and interior remodeling. The renovation is expected to be complete by the end of 2010.<sup id="cite_ref-64" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-64"><span>[</span>64<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <div style="clear:both;"></div> 
     <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Academics">Academics</span><span class="mw-editsection">[<a href="/w/index.php?title=University_of_North_Carolina_at_Chapel_Hill&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Academics">edit</a>]</span></h2> 
     <div class="thumb tleft"> 
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a href="/wiki/File:UNC_South_Building_.jpg" class="image"><img alt="A large building with many windows and four columns on the front." src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3d/UNC_South_Building_.jpg/220px-UNC_South_Building_.jpg" width="220" height="146" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3d/UNC_South_Building_.jpg/330px-UNC_South_Building_.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3d/UNC_South_Building_.jpg/440px-UNC_South_Building_.jpg 2x" /></a> 
       <div class="thumbcaption"> 
        <div class="magnify">
         <a href="/wiki/File:UNC_South_Building_.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"><img src="//bits.wikimedia.org/static-1.22wmf8/skins/common/images/magnify-clip.png" width="15" height="11" alt="" /></a>
        </div> South Building, administrative offices of the chancellor and College of Arts and Sciences
       </div> 
      </div> 
     </div> 
     <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Curriculum">Curriculum</span><span class="mw-editsection">[<a href="/w/index.php?title=University_of_North_Carolina_at_Chapel_Hill&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Curriculum">edit</a>]</span></h3> 
     <p>UNC offers 71 bachelor's, 107 master's and 74 <a href="/wiki/Doctorate" title="Doctorate">doctoral degree</a> programs.<sup id="cite_ref-compendium_65-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-compendium-65"><span>[</span>65<span>]</span></a></sup> The university enrolls more than 28,000 students from all 100 <a href="/wiki/List_of_counties_in_North_Carolina" title="List of counties in North Carolina">North Carolina counties</a>, the other 49 states, and 47 other countries. State law requires that the percentage of students from North Carolina in each <a href="/wiki/Freshman" title="Freshman">freshman</a> class meet or exceed 82%.<sup id="cite_ref-66" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-66"><span>[</span>66<span>]</span></a></sup> The student body consists of 17,981 undergraduate students and 10,935 graduate and professional students (as of Fall 2009).<sup id="cite_ref-67" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-67"><span>[</span>67<span>]</span></a></sup> Minorities comprise 30.8% of UNC's undergraduate population<sup id="cite_ref-68" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-68"><span>[</span>68<span>]</span></a></sup> and applications from international students have more than doubled in the last five years (from 702 in 2004 to 1,629 in 2009).<sup id="cite_ref-69" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-69"><span>[</span>69<span>]</span></a></sup> Eighty-nine percent of enrolling first year students in 2009 reported a GPA of 4.0 or higher on a weighted 4.0 scale.<sup id="cite_ref-admissions.unc.edu_70-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-admissions.unc.edu-70"><span>[</span>70<span>]</span></a></sup> UNC students are strong competitors for national and international scholarships. In 2009, two UNC seniors won <a href="/wiki/Rhodes_Scholarship" title="Rhodes Scholarship">Rhodes Scholarships</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-71" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-71"><span>[</span>71<span>]</span></a></sup> The most popular majors at UNC are Biology, Business Administration, Psychology, Journalism and Mass Communication, and Political Science.<sup id="cite_ref-admissions.unc.edu_70-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-admissions.unc.edu-70"><span>[</span>70<span>]</span></a></sup> UNC also offers 300 study abroad programs in 70 countries.<sup id="cite_ref-72" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-72"><span>[</span>72<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <p>At the undergraduate level, all students must fulfill a number of general education requirements as part of the Making Connections curriculum, which was introduced in 2006.<sup id="cite_ref-73" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-73"><span>[</span>73<span>]</span></a></sup> English, social science, history, foreign language, mathematics, and natural science courses are required of all students, ensuring that they receive a broad <a href="/wiki/Liberal_arts" title="Liberal arts" class="mw-redirect">liberal arts</a> education.<sup id="cite_ref-74" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-74"><span>[</span>74<span>]</span></a></sup> The university also offers a wide range of first year seminars for incoming freshmen.<sup id="cite_ref-75" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-75"><span>[</span>75<span>]</span></a></sup> After their second year, students move on to the College of Arts and Sciences, or choose an undergraduate professional school program within the schools of medicine, nursing, business, education, pharmacy, information and library science, public health, or journalism and mass communication.<sup id="cite_ref-76" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-76"><span>[</span>76<span>]</span></a></sup> Undergraduates are held to an eight-semester limit of study.<sup id="cite_ref-77" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-77"><span>[</span>77<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Department_of_Public_Policy">Department of Public Policy</span><span class="mw-editsection">[<a href="/w/index.php?title=University_of_North_Carolina_at_Chapel_Hill&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Department of Public Policy">edit</a>]</span></h3> 
     <p>The UNC Department of Public Policy is a <a href="/wiki/Public_policy" title="Public policy">public policy</a> program offering specializations in areas such as global health policy, education policy, tax policy, and <a href="/wiki/Social_Justice" title="Social Justice" class="mw-redirect">social justice</a>. The Department was established in 2001.</p> 
     <p>Established in 1979, the Curriculum in Public Policy Analysis was one of the first undergraduate degree programs in public policy, and a charter member of the national Association for Public Policy Analysis and Management. It was augmented in 1991 by an interdisciplinary PhD Curriculum in Public Policy Analysis. In 1995 the two curricula were combined and began recruiting their own core faculty. In 2001 the combined curriculum became the present Department of Public Policy.</p> 
     <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Honor_Code">Honor Code</span><span class="mw-editsection">[<a href="/w/index.php?title=University_of_North_Carolina_at_Chapel_Hill&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Honor Code">edit</a>]</span></h3> 
     <p>The university has a longstanding <a href="/wiki/Honor_Code" title="Honor Code" class="mw-redirect">Honor Code</a> known as the &quot;Instrument of Student Judicial Governance,&quot; supplemented by an entirely student-run Honor System to resolve issues with students accused of academic and conduct offenses against the university community.<sup id="cite_ref-78" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-78"><span>[</span>78<span>]</span></a></sup> The Honor System is divided into three branches: the Student Attorney General Staff, the Honor Court, and the Honor System Outreach. The Student Attorney General is appointed by the Student Body President to investigate all reports of Honor Code violations and determine whether or not to bring charges against the student as detailed in the &quot;Instrument.&quot; The Attorney General is supported by a select staff of around 40 students. The Honor Court is led by the Chair, who is appointed by the Student Body President, and supported by Vice Chairs who adjudicate all students' hearings. The Honor Court as a whole is made up of some 80 selected students. The Honor System Outreach is a branch of the System solely devoted to promoting honor and integrity in the University community. UNC is the only public university, with the exception of the military academies, that has a completely student-run system from the beginning to the end of the process.</p> 
     <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Libraries">Libraries</span><span class="mw-editsection">[<a href="/w/index.php?title=University_of_North_Carolina_at_Chapel_Hill&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Libraries">edit</a>]</span></h3> 
     <div class="thumb tright"> 
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a href="/wiki/File:2008-07-11_UNC-CH_Wilson_Library_1.jpg" class="image"><img alt="A large building with six Corinthian columns on the front with the sides being obscured by bushes." src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/07/2008-07-11_UNC-CH_Wilson_Library_1.jpg/220px-2008-07-11_UNC-CH_Wilson_Library_1.jpg" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/07/2008-07-11_UNC-CH_Wilson_Library_1.jpg/330px-2008-07-11_UNC-CH_Wilson_Library_1.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/07/2008-07-11_UNC-CH_Wilson_Library_1.jpg/440px-2008-07-11_UNC-CH_Wilson_Library_1.jpg 2x" /></a> 
       <div class="thumbcaption"> 
        <div class="magnify">
         <a href="/wiki/File:2008-07-11_UNC-CH_Wilson_Library_1.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"><img src="//bits.wikimedia.org/static-1.22wmf8/skins/common/images/magnify-clip.png" width="15" height="11" alt="" /></a>
        </div> 
        <a href="/wiki/Louis_Round_Wilson_Library" title="Louis Round Wilson Library">Louis Round Wilson Library</a> opened in 1929 and serves as the special collections library.
        <sup id="cite_ref-79" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-79"><span>[</span>79<span>]</span></a></sup>
       </div> 
      </div> 
     </div> 
     <p>UNC's library system includes a number of individual libraries housed throughout the campus and holds more than 7.0&nbsp;million volumes in total.<sup id="cite_ref-80" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-80"><span>[</span>80<span>]</span></a></sup> UNC's North Carolina Collection (NCC) is the largest and most comprehensive collection of holdings about any single state nationwide.<sup id="cite_ref-81" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-81"><span>[</span>81<span>]</span></a></sup> The unparalleled assemblage of literary, visual, and artifactual materials documents four centuries of North Carolina history and culture.<sup id="cite_ref-82" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-82"><span>[</span>82<span>]</span></a></sup> The North Carolina Collection is housed in <a href="/wiki/Wilson_Library" title="Wilson Library" class="mw-redirect">Wilson Library</a>, named after <a href="/wiki/Louis_Round_Wilson" title="Louis Round Wilson">Louis Round Wilson</a>, along with the Rare Books Collection and the Southern Folklife Collection.<sup id="cite_ref-83" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-83"><span>[</span>83<span>]</span></a></sup> The university is home to <a href="/wiki/Ibiblio" title="Ibiblio">ibiblio</a>, one of the world's largest collections of freely available information including software, music, literature, art, history, science, politics, and cultural studies.<sup id="cite_ref-84" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-84"><span>[</span>84<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-85" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-85"><span>[</span>85<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <p>The Davis Library, situated near the Pit, is the main library and the largest academic facility and state-owned building in North Carolina.<sup id="cite_ref-reflections_54-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-reflections-54"><span>[</span>54<span>]</span></a></sup> It was named after North Carolina philanthropist <a href="/wiki/Walter_R._Davis" title="Walter R. Davis">Walter Royal Davis</a> and opened on February 6, 1984. The first book checked out of Davis Library was <a href="/wiki/George_Orwell" title="George Orwell">George Orwell</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Nineteen_Eighty-Four" title="Nineteen Eighty-Four"><i>1984</i></a>.<sup id="cite_ref-86" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-86"><span>[</span>86<span>]</span></a></sup> The R.B. House Undergraduate Library is located between the Pit area and Wilson Library. It is named after Robert B. House, the Chancellor of UNC from 1945 to 1957, and it opened in 1968.<sup id="cite_ref-87" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-87"><span>[</span>87<span>]</span></a></sup> In 2001, the R.B. House Undergraduate Library underwent a $9.9 million renovation that modernized the furnishings, equipment, and infrastructure of the building.<sup id="cite_ref-88" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-88"><span>[</span>88<span>]</span></a></sup> Prior to the construction of Davis, Wilson Library was the university's main library, but now Wilson hosts special events and houses special collections, rare books, and temporary exhibits.<sup id="cite_ref-89" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-89"><span>[</span>89<span>]</span></a></sup></p> 
     <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Rankings_and_reputation">Rankings and reputation</span><span class="mw-editsection">[<a href="/w/index.php?title=University_of_North_Carolina_at_Chapel_Hill&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Rankings and reputation">edit</a>]</span></h3>
